				<div class="page-heading">
					<h1 id="section_0">Qualcomm</h1>
					<div class="tagline"></div>
				</div>

				<div class="minerva__subtitle"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=QUALCOMM_Inc.&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="QUALCOMM Inc.">QUALCOMM Inc.</a>)</span></div>
			</div>
			
			<div id="bodyContent" class="content"><div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="mw-parser-output"><section class="mf-section-0" id="mf-section-0">
<p><b>Qualcomm</b> is a public multinational corporation headquartered in <a href="/wiki/San_Diego,_California" class="mw-redirect" title="San Diego, California">San Diego, California</a>. It creates <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_property" title="Intellectual property">intellectual property</a>, semiconductors, software, and services related to wireless technology. It owns patents critical to the <a href="/wiki/CDMA2000" title="CDMA2000">CDMA2000</a>, <a href="/wiki/TD-SCDMA" class="mw-redirect" title="TD-SCDMA">TD-SCDMA</a> and <a href="/wiki/WCDMA" class="mw-redirect" title="WCDMA">WCDMA</a> mobile communications standards.
</p><table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><caption class="fn org">Qualcomm Incorporated</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" class="logo" style="text-align:center"><a McLaughlin, Chairman</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Products</th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;">Intellectual property and semiconductors</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" (2019)<sup id="cite_ref-10k_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10k-1">[1]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Asset" (2019)<sup id="cite_ref-10k_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10k-1">[1]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><div style="padding:0.1em href="#cite_note-10k-1">[1]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Website</th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="https://www.qualcomm.com">https://www.qualcomm.com</a></td></tr></tbody></table><p>Qualcomm was established in 1985 by Irwin Jacobs and six other co-founders. Its early research into <a href="/wiki/CDMA" class="mw-redirect" title="CDMA">CDMA</a> wireless cell phone technology was funded by selling satellite communications systems for commercial trucks. After a heated debate in the wireless industry, the 3G standard was adopted with Qualcomm's CDMA patents incorporated.<sup id="cite_ref-Chafkin_King_2017_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chafkin_King_2017-2">[2]</a></sup> Afterwards there was a series of legal disputes about pricing for licensing patents required by the standard.
</p><p>Over the years, Qualcomm has expanded into selling semiconductor products in a predominantly <a href="/wiki/Fabless_manufacturing" title="Fabless manufacturing">fabless manufacturing</a> model. It also developed semiconductor components or software for vehicles, watches, laptops, wi-fi, smartphones, and other devices.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Operations_and_market-share"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Operations and market-share</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>
</ul></div>

</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(1)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporate_history">Corporate history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Corporate history" data-section="1" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-1 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-1">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early history" data-section="2" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Qualcomm was created on July 1, 1985<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> by seven former <a href="/wiki/Linkabit" title="Linkabit">Linkabit</a> employees led by Irwin Jacobs.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup> The company was named Qualcomm for “QUALity COMMunications.”<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> It started as a contract research and development center<sup id="cite_ref-Steinbock2003_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steinbock2003-6">[6]</a></sup> largely for government and defense projects.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-six_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-six-7">[7]</a></sup></p><p>Qualcomm merged with <a href="/wiki/Omninet" title="Omninet">Omninet</a> in 1988 and raised $3.5 million in funding in order to produce the Omnitracs satellite communications system for trucking companies.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup> Qualcomm grew from eight employees in 1986 to 620 employees in 1991, due to demand for Omnitracs.<sup id="cite_ref-nytnyt_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytnyt-8">[8]</a></sup> By 1989, Qualcomm had $32 million in revenues, 50 percent of which was from an Omnitracs contract with <a href="/wiki/Schneider_National" title="Schneider National">Schneider National</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-two_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-two-9">[9]</a></sup> Omnitracs profits helped fund Qualcomm's research and development into <a href="/wiki/Code-division_multiple_access" title="Code-division multiple access">code-division multiple access</a> (CDMA) technologies for cell phone networks.<sup id="cite_ref-Steinbock2003_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steinbock2003-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tribunel_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tribunel-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="1990-2015">1990-2015</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1990-2015" data-section="3" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Qualcomm was operating at a loss in the 1990s due to its investment in CDMA research.<sup id="cite_ref-Steinbock2003_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steinbock2003-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tribunel_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tribunel-10">[10]</a></sup> To obtain funding, the company filed an <a href="/wiki/Initial_public_offering" title="Initial public offering">initial public offering</a> in September 1991<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> raising $68 million.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup> An additional $486 million was raised in 1995 through the sale of 11.5 million more shares. The second funding round was done to raise money for the mass manufacturing of CDMA-based phones, base-stations, and equipment, after most US-based cellular networks announced they would adopt the CDMA standard.<sup id="cite_ref-Steinbock2003_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steinbock2003-6">[6]</a></sup> The company had $383 million in annual revenue in 1995<sup id="cite_ref-bwbw_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwbw-12">[12]</a></sup> and $814 million by 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-bw3_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bw3-13">[13]</a></sup></p><p>In 1998, Qualcomm was restructured, leading to a 700-employee layoff. Its cell-phone manufacturing business was also spun-off in order to focus on its higher-margin patents business.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steinbock2003_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steinbock2003-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>310-311</span></sup> The following year, Qualcomm was the fastest growing stock on the market with a 2,621 percent growth over one year.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sandiegomag_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandiegomag-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> By 2000, Qualcomm had grown to 6,300 employees, $3.2 billion in revenues, and $670 million in profit. 39 percent of its sales were from CDMA technology, followed by licensing (22%), wireless (22%), and other products (17%).<sup id="cite_ref-Steinbock2003_6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steinbock2003-6">[6]</a></sup> Around this time, Qualcomm established offices in Europe, Asia Pacific, and Latin America.<sup id="cite_ref-Steinbock2003_6-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steinbock2003-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>316</span></sup> By 2001, 65 percent of Qualcomm's revenues originated from outside the United States with 35 percent coming from South Korea.<sup id="cite_ref-Steinbock2003_6-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steinbock2003-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>19</span></sup></p><p>In 2005, <a href="/wiki/Paul_E._Jacobs" title="Paul E. Jacobs">Paul E. Jacobs</a>, son of Qualcomm founder Dr. Irwin Jacobs, was appointed as Qualcomm's new CEO.<sup id="cite_ref-nytsy_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytsy-17">[17]</a></sup> Whereas Irwin Jacobs focused on CDMA patents, Paul Jacobs refocused much of Qualcomm's new research and development on projects related to the <a href="/wiki/Internet_of_things" title="Internet of things">internet of things</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nytsy_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytsy-17">[17]</a></sup> Qualcomm announced Steven Mollenkopf would succeed Paul Jacobs as CEO in December 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Mollenkopf said he would expand Qualcomm's focus to wireless technology for cars, wearable devices, and other new markets.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rubin_2014_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin_2014-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="NXP_and_Broadcom">NXP and Broadcom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: NXP and Broadcom" data-section="4" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Qualcomm announced its intent to acquire NXP Semiconductors for $47 billion in October 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-Fortune_2016_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fortune_2016-22">[22]</a></sup> The deal was approved by U.S. antitrust regulators in April 2017<sup id="cite_ref-Fortune_2017_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fortune_2017-23">[23]</a></sup> with some standard-essential patents excluded to get the deal approved by antitrust regulators.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Drozdiak_2018_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drozdiak_2018-25">[25]</a></sup></p><p>As the NXP acquisition was ongoing, Broadcom made a $103 billion offer to acquire Qualcomm,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mukherjee_2017_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mukherjee_2017-27">[27]</a></sup> and Qualcomm rejected the offer.<sup id="cite_ref-Mukherjee_20172_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mukherjee_20172-28">[28]</a></sup> Broadcom attempted a hostile takeover,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> and raised its offer, eventually to $121 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-dkahfad_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dkahfad-30">[30]</a></sup> The potential Broadcom acquisition was investigated by the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Committee_on_Foreign_Investment_in_the_United_States" title="Committee on Foreign Investment in the United States">Committee on Foreign Investment</a><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and blocked by an executive order from U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>, citing national security concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p><p>Qualcomm's NXP acquisition then became a part of the <a href="/wiki/2018_China%E2%80%93United_States_trade_war" class="mw-redirect" title="2018 China–United States trade war">2018 China–United States trade war</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ouafhouh_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ouafhouh-33">[33]</a></sup> U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> blocked China-based <a href="/wiki/ZTE_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="ZTE Corporation">ZTE Corporation</a> from buying American-made components, such as those from Qualcomm.<sup id="cite_ref-ouafhouh_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ouafhouh-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_New_York_Times_2018_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_New_York_Times_2018-34">[34]</a></sup> The ZTE restriction was lifted after the two countries reached an agreement,<sup id="cite_ref-Financial_Times_2017_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Financial_Times_2017-35">[35]</a></sup> but then Trump raised tariffs against Chinese goods.<sup id="cite_ref-ouafhouh_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ouafhouh-33">[33]</a></sup> Qualcomm extended a tender offer to NXP at least 29 times pending Chinese approval,<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters_2018_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters_2018-36">[36]</a></sup> before abandoning the deal in July 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(2)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Wireless_CDMA">Wireless CDMA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Wireless CDMA" data-section="5" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-2 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-2">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="2G">2G</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2G" data-section="6" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history_2">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Early history" data-section="7" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>In mid-1985, Qualcomm was hired by <a href="/wiki/Hughes_Aircraft" class="mw-redirect" title="Hughes Aircraft">Hughes Aircraft</a> to provide research and testing for a satellite network proposal to the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission" title="Federal Communications Commission">Federal Communications Commission</a> (FCC).<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>38</span></sup> The following year, Qualcomm filed its first CDMA patent (No. 4,901,307).<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup> This patent established Qualcomm's overall approach to CDMA<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup> and later became one of the most frequently cited technical documents in history.<sup id="cite_ref-BlaxillEckardt2009_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BlaxillEckardt2009-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>84</span></sup> The project with the FCC was scrapped in 1988, when the FCC told all twelve vendors that submitted proposals to form a joint venture to create a single proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>38</span></sup></p><p>Qualcomm further developed the CDMA techniques for commercial use and submitted them to the <a href="/wiki/Cellular_Telephone_Industries_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="Cellular Telephone Industries Association">Cellular Telephone Industries Association</a> (CTIA) in 1989 as an alternative to the <a href="/wiki/Time_division_multiple_access" class="mw-redirect" title="Time division multiple access">time division multiple access</a> (TDMA) standard for second-generation cell-phone networks.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>49</span></sup> A few months later, CTIA officially rejected Qualcomm's CDMA standard<sup id="cite_ref-LeiSlocum2013_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LeiSlocum2013-40">[40]</a></sup> in favor of the more established TDMA standard developed by <a href="/wiki/Ericsson" title="Ericsson">Ericsson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Steinbock2003_6-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steinbock2003-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bw3_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bw3-13">[13]</a></sup></p><p>At the time, CDMA wasn't considered viable in high-volume commercial applications due to the near-far field effect, whereby phones closer to a cell tower with a stronger signal drown out callers that are further away and have a weaker signal.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>54-55,62-65</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wsjalpha_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsjalpha-41">[41]</a></sup> Qualcomm filed three additional patents in 1989. They were for: a power management system that adjusts the signal strength of each call to adjust for the near-far field effect; a "soft handoff" methodology for transferring callers from one cell-tower to the next; and a variable rate encoder, which reduces bandwidth usage when a caller isn't speaking.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>54-55,62-65</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wsjalpha_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsjalpha-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Holy_wars_of_wireless">Holy wars of wireless</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Holy wars of wireless" data-section="8" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>After the FCC said carriers were allowed to implement standards not approved by the CTIA, Qualcomm began pitching its CDMA technology directly to carriers.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup> This started what is often referred to as "the Holy Wars of Wireless," an often heated debate about whether TDMA or CDMA was better suited for 2G networks.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>117-120</span></sup> Qualcomm-supported CDMA standards eventually unseated TDMA as the more popular 2G standard in North America, due to its network capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-bw3_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bw3-13">[13]</a></sup></p><p>Qualcomm conducted CDMA test demonstrations in 1989<sup id="cite_ref-Klemens2010_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klemens2010-42">[42]</a></sup> in San Diego and in 1990 in New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-thanks_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thanks-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chen2007_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chen2007-44">[44]</a></sup> In 1990, Nynex Mobile Communications and Ameritech Mobile Communications were the first carriers to implement CDMA networks instead of TDMA.<sup id="cite_ref-thanks_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thanks-43">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Motorola" title="Motorola">Motorola</a>, a prior TDMA advocate, conducted CDMA test implementations in <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wsjalpha_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsjalpha-41">[41]</a></sup> This was followed by a $2 million trial network in San Diego for Airtouch Communications.<sup id="cite_ref-bw3_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bw3-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chen2007_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chen2007-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>177</span></sup> In November 1991, 14 carriers and manufacturers conducted large-scale CDMA field tests.<sup id="cite_ref-bw3_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bw3-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-international_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-international-46">[46]</a></sup></p><p>Results from the test implementations convinced CTIA to re-open discussions regarding CDMA and the 2G standard.<sup id="cite_ref-PhD2010_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PhD2010-47">[47]</a></sup> CTIA changed its position and supported CDMA in 1993,<sup id="cite_ref-bw3_13-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bw3-13">[13]</a></sup> adopting Qualcomm's CDMA as the IS-95A standard, also known as cdmaOne.<sup id="cite_ref-LemstraHayes2010_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LemstraHayes2010-48">[48]</a></sup> This prompted widespread criticism in forums, trade press, and conventions from businesses that had already invested heavily in the TDMA standard and from TDMA's developer, Ericsson.<sup id="cite_ref-bw3_13-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bw3-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PhD2010_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PhD2010-47">[47]</a></sup></p><p>The first commercial-scale CDMA cellular network was created in Hong Kong in 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-LemstraHayes2010_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LemstraHayes2010-48">[48]</a></sup> On July 21, 1995, <a href="/wiki/Primeco" class="mw-redirect" title="Primeco">Primeco</a>, which represented a consortium of <a href="/wiki/Cox_Communications" title="Cox Communications">Cox Communications</a>, <a href="/wiki/Comcast" title="Comcast">Comcast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sprint_Corporation" title="Sprint Corporation">Sprint</a> and others, announced it was going to implement CDMA-based services<sup id="cite_ref-bw3_13-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bw3-13">[13]</a></sup> on networks in 15 states.<sup id="cite_ref-LemstraHayes2010_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LemstraHayes2010-48">[48]</a></sup> By this time, 11 out of 14 of the world's largest networks supported CDMA.<sup id="cite_ref-bw3_13-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bw3-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-economist_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist-49">[49]</a></sup> By 1997 CDMA had 57 percent of the US market, whereas 14 percent of the market was on TDMA.<sup id="cite_ref-bw3_13-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bw3-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="International">International</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: International" data-section="9" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>In 1991, Qualcomm and the <a href="/wiki/Electronics_and_Telecommunications_Research_Institute" title="Electronics and Telecommunications Research Institute">Electronics and Telecommunications Research Institute</a> (ETRI) agreed to jointly develop CDMA technologies for the Korean telecommunications infrastructure.<sup id="cite_ref-Reddick2012_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reddick2012-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pacific2004_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pacific2004-51">[51]</a></sup> A CDMA standard was adopted as the national wireless standard in Korea in May 1993<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup> with commercial CDMA networks being launched in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-Pacific2004_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pacific2004-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reddick2012_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reddick2012-50">[50]</a></sup> CDMA networks were also launched in Argentina, Brazil, Mexico, India, and Venezuela.<sup id="cite_ref-DrakeWilson2008_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DrakeWilson2008-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HarperBuress2008_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HarperBuress2008-53">[53]</a></sup> Qualcomm entered the Russian and Latin American markets in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup> By 2007, Qualcomm's technology was in cell phone networks in more than 105 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-DrakeWilson2008_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DrakeWilson2008-52">[52]</a></sup> Qualcomm also formed licensing agreements with <a href="/wiki/Nokia" title="Nokia">Nokia</a> in Europe, <a href="/wiki/Nortel" title="Nortel">Nortel</a> in Canada, and with <a href="/wiki/Matsushita" title="Matsushita">Matsushita</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi" title="Mitsubishi">Mitsubishi</a> in Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-Steinbock2003_6-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steinbock2003-6">[6]</a></sup></p><p>Qualcomm entered the Chinese market through a partnership with <a href="/wiki/China_Unicom" title="China Unicom">China Unicom</a> in 2000,<sup id="cite_ref-Steinbock2003_6-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steinbock2003-6">[6]</a></sup> which launched the first CDMA-based network in China in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-HarperBuress2008_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HarperBuress2008-53">[53]</a></sup> China became a major market for Qualcomm's semiconductor products, representing more than fifty percent of its revenues,<sup id="cite_ref-reutreur_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutreur-54">[54]</a></sup> but also the source of many legal disputes regarding Qualcomm's intellectual property.<sup id="cite_ref-wsjsj_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsjsj-55">[55]</a></sup> By 2007, $500 million of Qualcomm's annual revenues were coming from Korean manufacturers.<sup id="cite_ref-OhLarson2011_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OhLarson2011-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Manufacturing">Manufacturing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Manufacturing" data-section="10" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>Initially, Qualcomm's manufacturing operations were limited to a small ASIC design and manufacturing team to support the Omnitracs system.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup> Qualcomm was forced to expand into manufacturing in the 1990s in order to produce the hardware carriers needed to implement CDMA networks that used Qualcomm's intellectual property.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup> Qualcomm's first large manufacturing project was in May 1993, in a deal to provide 36,000 CDMA phones to <a href="/wiki/US_West" title="US West">US West</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chen2007_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chen2007-44">[44]</a></sup></p><p>For a time, Qualcomm experienced delays and other manufacturing problems, because it was in-experienced with mass manufacturing.<sup id="cite_ref-Steinbock2003_6-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steinbock2003-6">[6]</a></sup> In 1994, Qualcomm partnered with <a href="/wiki/Northern_Telecom" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Telecom">Northern Telecom</a> and formed a joint partnership with <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">Sony</a>, in order to leverage their manufacturing expertise.<sup id="cite_ref-Klemens2010_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klemens2010-42">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nokia" title="Nokia">Nokia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samsung" title="Samsung">Samsung</a> and <a href="/wiki/Motorola" title="Motorola">Motorola</a> introduced their own CDMA phones in 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-Klemens2010_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klemens2010-42">[42]</a></sup> Qualcomm's manufacturing business was losing money due to large capital equipment costs and declining prices caused by competition.<sup id="cite_ref-Klemens2010_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klemens2010-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BlaxillEckardt2009_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BlaxillEckardt2009-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup> Also, in March 1997, after Qualcomm introduced its Q phone, <a href="/wiki/Motorola" title="Motorola">Motorola</a> initiated a lawsuit (settled out of court in 2000) for allegedly copying the design of its Startac phone.<sup id="cite_ref-Inc.1997_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Inc.1997-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p><p>In December 1999, Qualcomm sold its manufacturing interests to Kyocera Corporation, a Japanese CDMA manufacturer and Qualcomm licensee.<sup id='cite_ref-“pike"_59-0' class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-%E2%80%9Cpike%22-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Qualcomm's infrastructure division was sold to competitor Ericsson in 1999 as part of an out-of-court agreement for a CDMA patent dispute that started in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-thirteen_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thirteen-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The sale of the infrastructure division marked the beginning of an increase in Qualcomm's stock price and stronger financial performance, but many of the 1,200 employees involved were discontent with working for a competitor and losing their stock options.<sup id="cite_ref-thirteen_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thirteen-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> This led to a protracted legal dispute regarding employee stock options, resulting in $74 million in settlements by 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="3G">3G</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 3G" data-section="11" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>3G standards were expected to force prior TDMA carriers onto CDMA, in order to meet 3G bandwidth goals.<sup id="cite_ref-BlaxillEckardt2009_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BlaxillEckardt2009-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Drake(III.)2008_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drake(III.)2008-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Drake(III.)2008_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drake(III.)2008-64">[64]</a></sup> The two largest GSM manufacturers, Nokia and Ericsson, advocated for a greater role for GSM,<sup id="cite_ref-TaplinWakui2006_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TaplinWakui2006-65">[65]</a></sup> in order to negotiate lower royalty prices from Qualcomm.<sup id="cite_ref-Drake(III.)2008_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drake(III.)2008-64">[64]</a></sup> In 1998, the <a href="/wiki/European_Telecommunications_Standards_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="European Telecommunications Standards Institute">European Telecommunications Standards Institute</a> (ETSI) voted in support of the WCDMA standard, which relied less on Qualcomm's CDMA patents.<sup id="cite_ref-Association2010_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Association2010-66">[66]</a></sup> Qualcomm responded by refusing to license its intellectual property for the standard.<sup id="cite_ref-Drake(III.)2008_64-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drake(III.)2008-64">[64]</a></sup></p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_Industry_Association" title="Telecommunications Industry Association">Telecommunications Industry Association</a> (TIA) and the Third Generation Partnership Program 2, advocated for a competing CDMA-2000 standard developed primarily by Qualcomm.<sup id="cite_ref-ChenGuizani2006_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChenGuizani2006-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Unhelkar2006_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Unhelkar2006-68">[68]</a></sup> American and European politicians advocated for the CDMA-2000 and WCDMA standards respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Drake(III.)2008_64-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drake(III.)2008-64">[64]</a></sup> The ITU said it would exclude Qualcomm's CDMA technology from the 3G standards entirely if a patent dispute over the technology with Ericsson was not resolved.<sup id="cite_ref-Mock2005_4-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mock2005-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lennon2007_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lennon2007-69">[69]</a></sup> The two reached an agreement out-of-court in 1999, one month before a deadline set by the ITU. Both companies agreed to cross-license their technology to each other<sup id="cite_ref-Lennon2007_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lennon2007-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>30</span></sup> and to work together on 3G standards.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p><p>A compromise was eventually reached whereby the ITU would initially endorse three standards: CDMA2000 1X, WCDMA and TD-SCDMA.<sup id="cite_ref-Drake(III.)2008_64-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drake(III.)2008-64">[64]</a></sup> Qualcomm agreed to license its CDMA patents for variants such as WCDMA.<sup id="cite_ref-Drake(III.)2008_64-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drake(III.)2008-64">[64]</a></sup> There were 240 million CDMA 3G subscribers by 2004 and 143 carriers in 67 countries by 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-Chen2007_44-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chen2007-44">[44]</a></sup> Qualcomm claimed to own 38 percent of WCDMA's essential patents, whereas European GSM interests sponsored a research paper allegigng Qualcomm only owned 19 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-BlaxillEckardt2009_39-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BlaxillEckardt2009-39">[39]</a></sup></p><p>Qualcomm consolidated its interests in telecommunications carriers, such as <a href="/wiki/Cricket_Communications" class="mw-redirect" title="Cricket Communications">Cricket Communications</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pegaso" title="Pegaso">Pegaso</a> into a holding company, <a href="/wiki/Leap_Wireless" title="Leap Wireless">Leap Wireless</a>, in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steinbock2003_6-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steinbock2003-6">[6]</a></sup> Leap was spun-off later that year<sup id="cite_ref-jkopys_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jkopys-72">[72]</a></sup> and sold to <a href="/wiki/AT%26T" title="AT&amp;T">AT&amp;T</a> in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-jkopys_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jkopys-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="4G">4G</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 4G" data-section="12" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Qualcomm initially advocated for the CDMA-based <a href="/wiki/Ultra_Mobile_Broadband" class="mw-redirect" title="Ultra Mobile Broadband">Ultra Mobile Broadband</a> (UMB) standard for fourth generation wireless networks.<sup id="cite_ref-Unhelkar2009_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Unhelkar2009-73">[73]</a></sup> UMB wasn't backwards compatible with prior CDMA networks and didn't operate as well in narrow bandwidths as the LTE (long-term evolution) standard. No cellular networks adopted UMB.<sup id="cite_ref-Cox2012_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cox2012-74">[74]</a></sup> Qualcomm halted development of UMB in 2005 and decided to support the LTE standard,<sup id="cite_ref-Korhonen2014_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korhonen2014-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> even though it didn't rely as heavily on Qualcomm patents.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Then, Qualcomm purchased LTE-related patents through acquisitions.<sup id="cite_ref-Coll2013_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coll2013-78">[78]</a></sup> By 2012, Qualcomm held 81 seminal patents used in 4G LTE standards, or 12.46 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-Reed_2012_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reed_2012-79">[79]</a></sup></p><p>Qualcomm also became more focused on using its intellectual property to manufacture semiconductors in a <a href="/wiki/Fabless_manufacturing" title="Fabless manufacturing">fabless manufacturing</a> model.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> A VLSI Technology Organization division was founded in 2004, followed by a DFX group in 2006, which did more of the manufacturing design in-house.<sup id="cite_ref-eetimesss_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eetimesss-81">[81]</a></sup> Qualcomm announced it was developing the <a href="/wiki/Scorpion_(CPU)" title="Scorpion (CPU)">Scorpion</a> <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">central processing unit</a> (CPU) for mobile devices in November 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-djabnf_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-djabnf-83">[83]</a></sup> This was followed by the first shipments of the <a href="/wiki/Snapdragon_(system_on_chip)" class="mw-redirect" title="Snapdragon (system on chip)">Snapdragon</a> system-on-chip product, which includes a CPU, GPS, graphics processing unit, camera support and other software and semiconductors,<sup id="cite_ref-extreme_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-extreme-84">[84]</a></sup> in November 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-cnet_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnet-85">[85]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gobi" class="mw-redirect" title="Gobi">Gobi</a> family of modems for portable devices was released in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-giz_announcement_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-giz_announcement-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-engadget_announcement_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-engadget_announcement-87">[87]</a></sup> Gobi modems were embedded in many laptop brands<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> and Snapdragon system on chips were embedded into most Android devices.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p><p>Qualcomm won a government auction in India in 2010 for $1 billion in spectrum and licenses from which to offer broadband services. It formed four joint ventures with Indian holding companies for this purpose. A 49 percent stake in the holding companies was acquired by <a href="/wiki/Bharti_Enterprises" title="Bharti Enterprises">Bharti</a> in May 2012 and the remaining was acquired in October 2012<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> by AT&amp;T.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="5G">5G</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 5G" data-section="13" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>According to <i>Fortune Magazine</i>, Qualcomm has been developing technologies for future 5G standards in three areas: radios that would use bandwidth from any network it has access to, creating larger ranges of spectrum by combining smaller pieces, and a set of services for <a href="/wiki/Internet_of_things" title="Internet of things">internet of things</a> applications.<sup id="cite_ref-Higginbotham_2015_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Higginbotham_2015-93">[93]</a></sup> Qualcomm's first 5G modem chip was announced in October 2016<sup id="cite_ref-Captain_2016_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Captain_2016-94">[94]</a></sup> and a prototype was demonstrated in October 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News_2017_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News_2017-95">[95]</a></sup> Qualcomm's first 5G antennas were announced in July 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_2018_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_2018-96">[96]</a></sup> As of 2018, Qualcomm has partnerships with 19 mobile device manufacturers and 18 carriers to commercialize 5G technology.<sup id="cite_ref-Horwitz_2018_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horwitz_2018-97">[97]</a></sup> By late 2019, several phone were being sold with Qualcomm's 5G technology incorporated.<sup id="cite_ref-Nellis_2019_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nellis_2019-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(3)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Software_and_other_technology">Software and other technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Software and other technology" data-section="14" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-3 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-3">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_software">Early software</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Early software" data-section="15" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Qualcomm acquired an email application called <a href="/wiki/Eudora_(email_client)" title="Eudora (email client)">Eudora</a> in 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-merc_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merc-99">[99]</a></sup> By 1996, Eudora was installed on 63 percent of PCs.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Outlook" title="Microsoft Outlook">Microsoft Outlook</a> eclipsed Eudora, since it was provided for free by default on Windows-based machines.<sup id="cite_ref-aplip_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aplip-101">[101]</a></sup> By 2003 Qualcomm's Eudora was the most popular alternative to Microsoft Outlook, but still had only a five percent share of the market.<sup id="cite_ref-alt_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alt-102">[102]</a></sup> Software development for Eudora was retired in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-aplip_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aplip-101">[101]</a></sup></p><p>In 2001, Qualcomm introduced <a href="/wiki/Binary_Runtime_Environment_for_Wireless" title="Binary Runtime Environment for Wireless">Brew</a>, a smartphone app development service<sup id="cite_ref-whatisbrew_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whatisbrew-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Etoh2005_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Etoh2005-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-daghniouhd_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-daghniouhd-105">[105]</a></sup> with APIs to access contacts, billing, app-stores, or multimedia on the phone.<sup id="cite_ref-Etoh2005_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Etoh2005-104">[104]</a></sup> South Korean carrier KTFreeTel was the first to adopt the Brew system in November 2001, followed by Verizon in March 2002<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> for its "Get it Now" program. There were 2.5 million Brew users by the end of 2002 and 73 million in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-Chen2007_44-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chen2007-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_technology">Other technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Other technology" data-section="16" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2004, Qualcomm created a <a href="/wiki/MediaFLO" title="MediaFLO">MediaFLO</a> subsidiary to bring its FLO (forward link only) specification to market. Qualcomm built an $800 million MediaFLO network of cell towers to supplement carrier networks with one that is designed for multimedia.<sup id="cite_ref-yugdtsuobdajf_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yugdtsuobdajf-107">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> In comparison to cellular towers that provide two-way communications with each cell phone individually, MediaFLO towers would broadcast multimedia content to mobile phones in a one-way broadcast.<sup id="cite_ref-dajobiurebv_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dajobiurebv-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-apdia_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apdia-110">[110]</a></sup> Qualcomm also sold FLO-based semiconductors and licenses.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p><p>Qualcomm created the FLO Forum standards group with 15 industry participants in July 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Verizon was the first carrier to partner with MediaFlo in December 2005<sup id="cite_ref-apdia_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apdia-110">[110]</a></sup> for its Verizon Wireless' V Cast TV,<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> which was followed by the AT&amp;T Mobile TV service a couple months later.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The MediaFlo service was launched on Super Bowl Sunday in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-unique13_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unique13-117">[117]</a></sup> Despite the interest the service got among carriers, it was unpopular among consumers.<sup id="cite_ref-unique13_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unique13-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gigaomyud_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gigaomyud-118">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-uniquename_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uniquename-119">[119]</a></sup> The service required users to pay for a subscription and have phones that were equipped with special semiconductors.<sup id="cite_ref-gigaomyud_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gigaomyud-118">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-uniquename_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uniquename-119">[119]</a></sup> The service was discontinued in 2011 and its spectrum was sold to AT&amp;T for $1.93 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-gigaomyud_118-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gigaomyud-118">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-uniquename_119-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uniquename-119">[119]</a></sup> Qualcomm rebooted the effort in 2013 with LTE Broadcast, which uses pre-existing cell towers to broadcast select content locally on a dedicated spectrum, such as during major sporting events.<sup id="cite_ref-gigaomyud_118-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gigaomyud-118">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p><p>Based on technology acquired from Iridigm in 2004 for $170 million,<sup id="cite_ref-cnn_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-121">[121]</a></sup> Qualcomm began commercializing <a href="/wiki/Interferometric_modulator_display" title="Interferometric modulator display">Mirasol</a> displays in 2007, which was expanded into eight products in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Mirasol uses natural light shining on a screen to provide lighting for the display, rather a backlight, in order to reduce power consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> The amount of space between the surface of the display and a mirror within a 10 micron-wide "interferometric modulator" determines the color of the reflected light.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Mirasol was eventually closed down after an attempt to revive it in 2013 in Toq watches.<sup id="cite_ref-Captain_2017_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Captain_2017-125">[125]</a></sup></p><p>In June 2011, Qualcomm introduced <a href="/wiki/AllJoyn" title="AllJoyn">AllJoyn</a>, a wireless standard for communicating between devices like cell phones, televisions, air-conditioners, and refrigerators.<sup id="cite_ref-alljoynnww_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alljoynnww-126">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-oneilyady_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oneilyady-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FitzekKatz2013_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FitzekKatz2013-128">[128]</a></sup> The Alljoyn technology was donated to the <a href="/wiki/Linux_Foundation" title="Linux Foundation">Linux Foundation</a> in December 2013. Qualcomm and the Linux Foundation then formed the Allseen Alliance to administer the standard<sup id="cite_ref-alljoynnww_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alljoynnww-126">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> and Qualcomm developed products that used the AllJoyn standard<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> In December 2011, Qualcomm formed a healthcare subsidiary called Qualcomm Life. Simultaneously, the subsidiary released a cloud-based service for managing clinical data called 2net and the Qualcomm Life Fund, which invests in wireless healthcare technology companies.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> The subsidiary doubled its employee-count by acquiring HealthyCircles Inc., a healthcare IT company, the following May.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Qualcomm life was later sold to a private equity firm, Francisco Partners, in 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-MobiHealthNews_2019_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MobiHealthNews_2019-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_developments">Recent developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Recent developments" data-section="17" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2016, Qualcomm developed its first beta processor chip for servers and PCs called “Server Development Platform” and sent samples for testing.<sup id="cite_ref-dalihgeahrocin_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dalihgeahrocin-137">[137]</a></sup> In January 2017, a second generation data center and PC server chip called Centriq 2400 was released.<sup id="cite_ref-dalihgeahrocin_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dalihgeahrocin-137">[137]</a></sup> <i>PC Magazine</i> said the release was "historic" for Qualcomm, because it was a new market segment for the company.<sup id="cite_ref-Shah_2016_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shah_2016-138">[138]</a></sup> Qualcomm also created a Qualcomm Datacenter Technologies subsidiary to focus on the PCs and servers market.<sup id="cite_ref-eWeek.com_2016_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eWeek.com_2016-139">[139]</a></sup> In 2017, Qualcomm introduced embedded technology for 3D cameras intended for augmented reality apps.<sup id="cite_ref-Captain_2017b_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Captain_2017b-140">[140]</a></sup> Qualcomm is also developing and demonstrating laptop processors and other parts, as of 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p><p>In 2000, Qualcomm formed a joint venture with <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">FORD</a> called Wingcast, which created <a href="/wiki/Telematics" title="Telematics">telematics</a> equipment for cars, but was unsuccessful and closed down two years later.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> Qualcomm acquired the wireless electric car charging company, HaloIPT, in November 2011<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> and later sold the company to WiTricity in February 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-Sawers_2019_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sawers_2019-146">[146]</a></sup> Qualcomm also started introducing Snapdragon system-on-chips<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> and Gobi modems<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> and other software or semiconductor products for self-driving cars and modern in-car computers.<sup id="cite_ref-Velazco_2017_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Velazco_2017-149">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Condon_2017_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Condon_2017-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(4)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Patents_and_patent_disputes">Patents and patent disputes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Patents and patent disputes" data-section="18" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-4 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-4">
<p>As of 2017, Qualcomm owns more than 130,000 current or pending patents.<sup id="cite_ref-Chafkin_King_2017_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chafkin_King_2017-2">[2]</a></sup> By the early 2000s, Qualcomm had more than 1,000 patents.<sup id="cite_ref-eight_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eight-151">[151]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> As the sole early investor in CDMA research and development, Qualcomm's patent portfolio contains much of the intellectual property that is essential to CDMA technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-Klemens2010_42-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klemens2010-42">[42]</a></sup></p><p>Since many of Qualcomm's patents are part of an industry standard, the company has agreed to license those patents under “fair, reasonable, and non-discriminatory” terms.<sup id="cite_ref-Pressman_2017_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pressman_2017-153">[153]</a></sup> Qualcomm's royalties come out to about 5% or $30 per mobile device.<sup id="cite_ref-Chafkin_King_2017_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chafkin_King_2017-2">[2]</a></sup> According to Fortune Magazine, this is about 5-10 times more than what is typically charged by other patent-holders.<sup id="cite_ref-Pressman_2017_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pressman_2017-153">[153]</a></sup> Qualcomm says its patents are more expensive because they are more important and its pricing is within the range of common licensing practices.<sup id="cite_ref-Pressman_2017_153-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pressman_2017-153">[153]</a></sup> However, competitors, clients, and regulators often allege Qualcomm charges unreasonable rates or engages in unfair competition for mandatory patents.<sup id="cite_ref-Delta2009_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Delta2009-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Broadcom">Broadcom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Broadcom" data-section="19" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2005, Broadcom and Qualcomm were unable to reach an agreement on cross-licensing their intellectual property,<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> and Broadcom sued Qualcomm alleging it was breach ten Broadcom patents.<sup id="cite_ref-wsjwsjwsjjj_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsjwsjwsjjj-156">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mei2012_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mei2012-157">[157]</a></sup> Broadcom asked the International Trade Commission to prohibit importing the affected technology.<sup id="cite_ref-wsjwsjwsjjj_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsjwsjwsjjj-156">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> A separate lawsuit alleged Qualcomm was threatening to withhold UMTS patent licenses against manufacturers that bought their semiconductors from competitors, in violation of the standards agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-Stern2005_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2005-159">[159]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stern2005_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2005-159">[159]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p><p>Qualcomm alleged Broadcom was using litigation as a negotiation tactic and that it would respond with its own lawsuits.<sup id="cite_ref-Stern2005_159-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2005-159">[159]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> Qualcomm sued Broadcom, alleging it was using seven Qualcomm patents without permission.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> By late 2006, more than 20 lawsuits had been filed between the two parties and both sides claimed to be winning.<sup id="cite_ref-bwuyda_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwuyda-164">[164]</a></sup></p><p>In September 2006, a New Jersey court judge ruled that Qualcomm's patent monopoly was an inherit aspect of creating industry standards and that Qualcomm's pricing practices were lawful.<sup id="cite_ref-bwuyda_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwuyda-164">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stern2006_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2006-165">[165]</a></sup> In May 2007, a jury ordered Qualcomm to pay Broadcom $19.6 million for infringing on three Broadcom patents.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> In June 2007, the ITC ruled that Qualcomm had infringed on at least one Broadcom patent and banned corresponding imports.<sup id="cite_ref-Mei2012_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mei2012-157">[157]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Qualcomm and Broadcom reached a settlement in April 2009, resulting in a cross-licensing agreement, a dismissal of all litigation and Qualcomm paying $891 million over four years.<sup id="cite_ref-julione_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-julione-168">[168]</a></sup></p><p>During the litigation, Qualcomm claimed it had never participated in the JVT standards-setting process.<sup id="cite_ref-handbook_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-169">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>153</span></sup> However, an engineer's testimony led to discovery of 21 JVT-related email Qualcomm lawyers had withheld from the court and 200,000 pages of JVT-related documents.<sup id="cite_ref-BagleySavage2009_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BagleySavage2009-170">[170]</a></sup> Qualcomm's lawyers said the evidence was overlooked by accident, whereas the judge said it was gross misconduct.<sup id="cite_ref-BagleySavage2009_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BagleySavage2009-170">[170]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> Qualcomm was fined $8.5 million for legal misconduct.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> On appeal, the court held that Qualcomm could only enforce the related patents against non-JVT members, based on the agreements signed to participate in JVT.<sup id="cite_ref-handbook_169-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-169">[169]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BlockEpstein1995_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BlockEpstein1995-174">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>7</span></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Nokia_and_Project_Stockholm">Nokia and Project Stockholm</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Nokia and Project Stockholm" data-section="20" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Six large telecommunications companies<sup id="cite_ref-randommm_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-randommm-175">[175]</a></sup> led by Nokia<sup id="cite_ref-goodsource_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goodsource-176">[176]</a></sup> filed a complaint against Qualcomm with the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>'s antitrust division<sup id="cite_ref-goodsource_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goodsource-176">[176]</a></sup> in October 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> They alleged Qualcomm was abusing its market position to charge unreasonable rates for its patents.<sup id="cite_ref-DodgsonGann2013_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DodgsonGann2013-178">[178]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lundqvist2014_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lundqvist2014-179">[179]</a></sup> Qualcomm alleged the six companies were colluding together under the code name Project Stockholm in a legal strategy to negotiate lower rates.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-battle_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-battle-182">[182]</a></sup> These events led to a protracted legal dispute.<sup id="cite_ref-randommm_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-randommm-175">[175]</a></sup></p><p>Qualcomm filed a series of patent-infringement lawsuits against Nokia in Europe, Asia, the United States, and with the ITC.<sup id="cite_ref-post_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-post-183">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-timeline_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-184">[184]</a></sup> The parties initiated more than one dozen lawsuits against one another.<sup id="cite_ref-timeline_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-184">[184]</a></sup> Several companies filed antitrust complaints against Qualcomm with the Korean <a href="/wiki/Fair_Trade_Commission_(South_Korea)" title="Fair Trade Commission (South Korea)">Fair Trade Commission</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> who initiated an investigation into Qualcomm's practices in December 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-ten_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ten-186">[186]</a></sup> The dispute between Qualcomm and Nokia escalated, when their licensing agreement ended in April 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-nokia_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nokia-187">[187]</a></sup></p><p>In February 2008, the two parties agreed to halt any new litigation until an initial ruling is made on the first lawsuit in Delaware.<sup id="cite_ref-BagleySavage2009_170-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BagleySavage2009-170">[170]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-timeline_184-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-184">[184]</a></sup> Nokia won three consecutive court rulings with the German Federal Patent Court, the High Court in the United Kingdom, and the International Trade Commission respectively. Each found that Nokia was not infringing on Qualcomm's patents.<sup id="cite_ref-goodsource_176-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goodsource-176">[176]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lundqvist2014_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lundqvist2014-179">[179]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> In July 2008, Nokia and Qualcomm reached an out-of-court settlement that ended the dispute and created a 15-year cross-licensing agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-goodsource_176-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goodsource-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_disputes">Recent disputes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Recent disputes" data-section="21" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>ParkerVision filed a lawsuit against Qualcomm in July 2011 alleging that it infringed on seven ParkerVision patents related to converting electromagnetic radio signals to lower frequencies.<sup id="cite_ref-parker_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parker-189">[189]</a></sup> A $173 million jury verdict against Qualcomm was overturned by a judge.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup></p><p>In November 2013, the China National Development and Reform Commission initiated an anti-trust investigation into Qualcomm's licensing division.<sup id="cite_ref-reutreur_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutreur-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wsjsj_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsjsj-55">[55]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Securities and Exchange Commission">Securities and Exchange Commission</a> also started an investigation into whether Qualcomm breached antibribery laws through its activities in China.<sup id="cite_ref-wsjsj_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsjsj-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> The Chinese regulator raided Qualcomm's Chinese offices in August 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-reutersssst_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutersssst-192">[192]</a></sup> The dispute was settled in 2015 for $975 million.<sup id="cite_ref-Clark_2015_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark_2015-193">[193]</a></sup> In late 2016 The Korea Fair Trade Commission alleged Qualcomm abused a "dominant market position" to charge cell phone manufacturers excessive royalties for patents and limit sales to companies selling competing semiconductor products.<sup id="cite_ref-Fortune_2016hsj_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fortune_2016hsj-194">[194]</a></sup> The regulator gave Qualcomm a fine of $854 million, which the company said it will appeal.<sup id="cite_ref-Fortune_2016hsj_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fortune_2016hsj-194">[194]</a></sup></p><p>In April 2017, Qualcomm paid a $814.9 million settlement with <a href="/wiki/Blackberry" title="Blackberry">Blackberry</a> as a refund for prepaid licensing fees.<sup id="cite_ref-Cao_2017_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cao_2017-195">[195]</a></sup> In October 2017, Taiwan's Fair Trade Commission fined Qualcomm another $773 million.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> However, in late 2018 Qualcomm paid a settlement to Taiwan for $93 million in fines and a promise to spend $700 million in the local Taiwan economy.<sup id="cite_ref-Mickle_2018_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mickle_2018-197">[197]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lee_2018_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lee_2018-198">[198]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Apple">Apple</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Apple" data-section="22" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In January 2017, the Federal Trade Commission initiated an investigation into allegations that Qualcomm charged excessive royalties for patents that are "essential to industry standards."<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> That same year, Apple initiated a $1 billion lawsuit against Qualcomm in the U.S. alleging Qualcomm overcharged for semiconductors and failed to pay $1 billion in rebates.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> Apple also filed lawsuits in China<sup id="cite_ref-Pressman_2017_153-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pressman_2017-153">[153]</a></sup> and the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-adfljajdcpajdpfijaoiwec_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adfljajdcpajdpfijaoiwec-202">[202]</a></sup></p><p>Apple alleged Qualcomm was engaging in unfair competition by selling industry-standard patents at a discount rate in exchange for an exclusivity agreement for its semiconductor products.<sup id="cite_ref-King_McLaughlin_Rosenblatt_2017_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King_McLaughlin_Rosenblatt_2017-203">[203]</a></sup> An FTC report reached similar conclusions.<sup id="cite_ref-King_McLaughlin_Rosenblatt_2017_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King_McLaughlin_Rosenblatt_2017-203">[203]</a></sup> Qualcomm filed counter-claims alleging Apple made false and misleading statements to induce regulators to sue Qualcomm.<sup id="cite_ref-Forbespatents_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forbespatents-204">[204]</a></sup> Qualcomm also sued Apple's suppliers for allegedly not paying Qualcomm's patent royalties, after Apple stopped reimbursing them for patent fees.<sup id="cite_ref-Forbespatents_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forbespatents-204">[204]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fortune_2017Apple_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fortune_2017Apple-205">[205]</a></sup> Qualcomm petitioned the International Trade Commission to prohibit imports of iPhones, on the premise that they contain stolen Qualcomm patents after Apple's suppliers stopped paying.<sup id="cite_ref-King_Decker_2017_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King_Decker_2017-206">[206]</a></sup></p><p>In August, the International Trade Commission responded to Qualcomm's complaints by starting an investigation of Apple's use of Qualcomm patents without royalties.<sup id="cite_ref-Heavey_Nellis_2017_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heavey_Nellis_2017-207">[207]</a></sup> Qualcomm also filed suit against Apple in China for alleged patent infringement in October 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> The following month, Apple counter-sued, alleging Qualcomm was using patented Apple technology in its Android components.<sup id="cite_ref-Nellis_2017_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nellis_2017-209">[209]</a></sup></p><p>In December 2018 Chinese<sup id="cite_ref-Nellis_2018_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nellis_2018-210">[210]</a></sup> and Germany<sup id="cite_ref-Feiner_2018_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feiner_2018-211">[211]</a></sup> courts held that Apple infringed on Qualcomm patents and banned sales of certain iPhones. Some patents were held to be invalid,<sup id="cite_ref-Bloomberg_2019_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg_2019-212">[212]</a></sup> while others were infringed by Apple.<sup id="cite_ref-Nieva_2019_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nieva_2019-213">[213]</a></sup></p><p>In April 2019, Apple and Qualcomm reached an agreement to cease all litigation and sign a six-year licensing agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-daoijdaiew_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-daoijdaiew-214">[214]</a></sup> The settlement included a one-time payment from Apple of about $4.5 to $4.7 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-daiuowbavinadsewmgn_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-daiuowbavinadsewmgn-215">[215]</a></sup> Terms of the six-year licensing agreement were not disclosed, but the licensing fees were expected to increase revenues by $2 per-share.<sup id="cite_ref-daoijdaiew_214-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-daoijdaiew-214">[214]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-daiuowbavinadsewmgn_215-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-daiuowbavinadsewmgn-215">[215]</a></sup></p><p>In January 2018, the European Competition Commission fined Qualcomm $1.2 billion for an arrangement to use Qualcomm chips exclusively in Apple’s mobile products.<sup id="cite_ref-Olson_2018_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olson_2018-216">[216]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-danonqveroidnsiun_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-danonqveroidnsiun-217">[217]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Drozdiak_Greenwald_2018_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drozdiak_Greenwald_2018-218">[218]</a></sup> Qualcomm is appealing the decision.<sup id="cite_ref-Olson_2018_216-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olson_2018-216">[216]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-danonqveroidnsiun_217-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-danonqveroidnsiun-217">[217]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Drozdiak_Greenwald_2018_218-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drozdiak_Greenwald_2018-218">[218]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(5)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Operations_and_market-share">Operations and market-share</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Qualcomm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Operations and market-share" data-section="23" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-5 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-5">
<p>Qualcomm develops software, semiconductor designs, patented intellectual property, development tools and services, but does not manufacture physical products like phones or infrastructure equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-Kodama2012_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kodama2012-219">[219]</a></sup> The company's revenues are derived from licensing fees for use of its intellectual property, sales of semiconductor products that are based on its designs, and from other wireless hardware, software or services.<sup id="cite_ref-primary_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-primary-220">[220]</a></sup></p><p>Qualcomm divides its business into three categories:<sup id="cite_ref-primary2_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-primary2-221">[221]</a></sup></p>
